  - [18:05:12 - 18:06:52] **Crisis Management vs. Foresighting**

    If you are thoroughly familiar with last week's content, let's try answering these questions I formulated just now. Let's tackle them individually. Is foresighting the exact same thing as crisis management? What do you all think? No? If the answer is no, why is it not crisis management? Yes, Kai, welcome back. How was your trip? Good? Okay, Kai says that crisis management is more about looking immediately at the situation or the company right after an event occurs. It is focused on acute problem-solving. Foresighting, on the other hand, looks at the process and the probability of what might actually pan out in the near future, perhaps over a ten-year horizon. So, as Kai accurately points out, foresighting looks at the long-term tail of events, whereas crisis management is very acute. Those are excellent points. Crisis management is indeed an acute problem-solving approach to immediately pressing problems. Foresighting is fundamentally long-term. You are still trying to solve a problem, but the problem is futuristic. Crisis management is inherently reactive; you are reacting to a fire that has already started. Foresighting is all about being proactive. Furthermore, foresighting isn't just about mitigating threats; it is equally about identifying and addressing future opportunities. A crisis response is almost entirely focused on mitigating short-term threats, not hunting for opportunities. Is that distinction clear? Great, Kai nailed that one.

    如果大家对上周的内容已经很熟悉了，让我们来尝试回答我刚刚准备的这些问题。我们一个个来。技术远见（Foresighting）和危机管理（Crisis Management）是完全一样的吗？大家怎么看？不是？如果不是，为什么它不是危机管理？好，Kai，欢迎回来，你的旅行怎么样？很好？好的。Kai 认为危机管理更多的是在事件发生后，立即关注局势或公司本身的状况。它侧重于解决突发的、紧迫的问题。另一方面，技术远见着眼于过程，以及在不久的将来（也许是十年内）可能实际发生的事情的概率。所以，正如 Kai 准确指出的那样，技术远见关注的是事件的长期发展，而危机管理是非常急迫的。这些观点非常好。危机管理确实是针对眼前紧迫问题的一种急性问题解决方法。技术远见从根本上来说是长期的。你仍然是在试图解决问题，但这个问题是未来才会发生的。危机管理本质上是被动的；你是在对已经燃起的大火做出反应。而技术远见则是关于主动出击。此外，技术远见不仅仅是关于减轻威胁；它同样关乎识别和抓住未来的机遇。危机应对几乎完全集中在减轻短期威胁上，而不是寻找机会。这个区别清楚了吗？很好，Kai 完美地回答了这个问题。

  - [18:06:52 - 18:08:27] **The Scope of Technology Intelligence**

    Moving on to the next concept. Is technology intelligence and foresighting a purely technological exercise? I ask this because if you were to Google "technology intelligence" right now, you might get a narrow definition. However, in the discussion forum, many of you rightly brought up concepts like competitor intelligence and social intelligence, creating many interesting threads focused on those broader aspects. So, is technology intelligence strictly limited to the technology itself? Exactly, it is not. If you listened attentively during our first class, I explained that it used to be just about technology back when the scope of strategic planning was narrow and the time horizon was short. But today, when the time horizon is long and the strategic scope is widened, you absolutely need to look at the market. What else must you look at apart from technology and the market? You must look at social factors and environmental factors. What else? Behavioral factors. Think back to the project we did for Prof Tan regarding the classroom environment; that was entirely about understanding behavioral shifts in learning. What else is there? There is one specific topic I want you all to point out because it connects closely to our previous discussions. Yes, ethics. Ethics plays an incredibly important role in technology intelligence. Many people erroneously exclude ethics from technology assessment, but unfortunately, that is a massive oversight. It must be part and parcel of the process. I am sure many of you studying AI have taken courses on AI ethics. Think about how foundational AI ethics is to the deployment of AI as a whole.

    继续下一个概念。技术情报（Technology Intelligence）和技术远见纯粹只是一项关于技术的活动吗？我问这个问题是因为，如果你现在去谷歌搜索“技术情报”，你可能会得到一个狭义的定义。然而，在讨论区中，你们很多人正确地提出了竞争对手情报和社会情报等概念，并围绕这些更广泛的方面展开了许多有趣的讨论。那么，技术情报真的仅仅局限于技术本身吗？完全正确，并非如此。如果你在第一节课上专心听讲了，我就解释过，在过去，当战略规划的范围很窄、时间跨度很短的时候，它确实只关注技术。但在今天，当时间跨度变长、战略范围扩大时，你绝对需要关注市场。除了技术和市场，你还需要关注什么？你必须关注社会因素和环境因素。还有什么？行为因素。回想一下我们为 Tan 教授做的关于课堂环境的项目；那完全是为了理解学习行为的转变。还有什么？有一个特定的话题我希望大家指出来，因为它与我们之前的讨论密切相关。是的，伦理（Ethics）。伦理在技术情报中扮演着极其重要的角色。许多人错误地将伦理排除在技术评估之外，遗憾的是，这是一个巨大的疏忽。它必须是整个过程中不可或缺的一部分。我相信你们中很多学习 AI 的同学都上过 AI 伦理课。想想看，AI 伦理对于 AI 整体的部署有多么基础和关键。

  - [18:08:27 - 18:13:21] **Visionary Leaders and the Delphi Method**

    Next question: Is foresighting simply about a single visionary leader sitting at the top, handing down directions and speculations? There was a great forum post on this specific dynamic by Rini. Where is Rini? Okay, great. Rini posted about whether foresighting is just a top-down directive from a visionary leader. Let's discuss that. One student pointed out that while a strategic vision often comes from top management, the actual foresighting process is better initiated by R&D management or business unit leaders on the ground, though they obviously need higher management support to execute it. That is a very accurate answer. It should involve insights from R&D and other operational managers. Another student added, building on our last class, that foresighting can and should be a bottom-up approach where those doing the actual working and researching realize what the emerging trends are. That is also true. As I emphasized before, foresighting is a comprehensive process, and that process requires involving all kinds of stakeholders, exactly as Carlson's article detailed. If an organization relies solely on one visionary leader giving all the direction, there is no harm—provided that leader is an absolute, infallible genius. But in reality, that seldom happens. Rini's post critically examined what happens when an organization is entirely driven by one leader. What happens when that leader leaves? Or worse, what if that one leader is deeply flawed, highly commanding, and steering the company in completely the wrong direction while the rest of the industry is moving differently? It is true that many companies, especially Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs), operate this way because they simply lack the bandwidth or resources to engage in formal, broad-based foresighting activities. The leader decides, and the company follows. But does that mean formal foresighting ignores visionaries? No. In formal foresighting, there is a prominent technique called the Delphi method, which we will learn about in detail after the midterm break. The Delphi method is all about systematically seeking the opinions of industry leaders and experts. But crucially, it is not just one leader. You consult many leaders across the industry. You collect both quantitative and qualitative evidence to iteratively decide how the industry is likely to evolve. You absolutely need to talk to visionaries and leaders, but you do not let one single person direct the entire narrative. You use structured processes to seek consensus even among the experts. So, to conclude on ethics from earlier, someone correctly noted that applying ethics allows for transparency and helps break down barriers, ensuring that everyone is agreeable on the fundamental principles of what should or should not be developed. The question is, where does ethics come into the picture? Initial stages, middle stages, or later stages? Some might think the initial stage is too early to apply ethical constraints, but in reality, you need an ethical direction set from the very beginning, even if detailed ethical assessments happen a bit later as the technology's path becomes clearer. Okay, Seng Meng is here, and I don't want to eat into his time any further. I will address some of the remaining forum pointers later. Let's welcome Seng Meng. He is an incredibly busy person, constantly traveling and working, but he still made his way here today to share his insights. Thank you so much, Seng Meng. Please, let's get you set up. You want to use your laptop, right? Here is the HDMI cable.

    下一个问题：技术远见仅仅是指一位高高在上的有远见的领导者，向下传达指示和预测吗？关于这种特定的动态，Rini 在论坛上发了一篇很好的帖子。Rini 在哪里？好的，很好。Rini 帖子的主题是：技术远见是否仅仅是来自有远见的领导者的自上而下的指令。我们来讨论一下。一位同学指出，虽然战略愿景通常来自最高管理层，但实际的技术远见流程最好由一线的研发管理层或业务部门领导来发起，当然，他们显然需要高层管理的支持来执行。这是一个非常准确的答案。它应该包含来自研发和其他运营管理者的洞察。另一位同学补充说，基于我们上一节课的内容，技术远见可以也应该是一种自下而上的方法，让那些进行实际工作和研究的人意识到新兴趋势是什么。这也是对的。正如我之前强调的，技术远见是一个综合性的过程，这个过程需要所有相关利益者的参与，这正是 Carlson 文章中详细说明的。如果一个组织仅仅依赖一位有远见的领导者来指明所有方向，这并没有什么坏处——前提是这位领导者是一个绝对不会犯错的超级天才。但在现实中，这种情况很少发生。Rini 的帖子批判性地探讨了当一个组织完全由一位领导者驱动时会发生什么。当那位领导者离开时会怎样？或者更糟的是，如果这位领导者有着严重的缺陷，非常专横，并且在整个行业都在朝不同方向发展时，把公司引向了完全错误的方向，那该怎么办？确实，许多公司，特别是中小企业（SMEs），是按照这种方式运作的，因为他们根本没有精力或资源来参与正式的、广泛的技术远见活动。领导者做决定，公司就照做。但这是否意味着正式的技术远见会忽略有远见的人呢？不。在正式的技术远见中，有一种重要的方法叫做德尔菲法（Delphi method），我们将在期中考试后详细学习。德尔菲法的核心是系统地寻求行业领导者和专家的意见。但关键是，它不仅仅是一位领导者。你需要咨询行业内的多位领导者。你收集定量和定性的证据，通过迭代来决定行业可能如何演变。你绝对需要与有远见的人和领导者交流，但你不能让某一个人主导整个方向。你需要使用结构化的流程，在专家之间寻求共识。那么，回到早些时候关于伦理的结论，有人正确地指出，应用伦理可以提高透明度，有助于打破壁垒，确保每个人都在“应该开发什么”或“不应该开发什么”的基本原则上达成一致。问题是，伦理应该在哪个阶段介入？初始阶段、中间阶段还是后期阶段？有些人可能认为在初始阶段施加伦理约束为时过早，但实际上，你需要从一开始就设定一个伦理方向，即使详细的伦理评估是在技术路径变得更清晰之后的稍晚阶段进行的。好的，Seng Meng 到了，我不想再占用他的时间了。我稍后会处理论坛上剩下的一些问题。让我们欢迎 Seng Meng。他是一个非常忙碌的人，经常出差和工作，但他今天还是特意赶来分享他的见解。非常感谢你，Seng Meng。请进，我们来帮你设置一下。你想用你的笔记本电脑，对吧？这是 HDMI 线。

  - [18:20:02 - 18:23:43] **AI Singapore as an Innovation Intermediary**

    So, what does it mean to do foresighting at a national level? It means that a lot of the initiatives we completed, and the things we are executing right now at AI Singapore, are essentially exercises in technology forecasting. I often joke when someone asks me what life in the Singapore public sector is like: I tell them we are professional crystal ballers. We try to gaze into the crystal ball as much and as accurately as we can. We ingest massive amounts of information—from patent studies, from intense dialogues with various industries, from collecting raw data—because our job is to steer the nation of Singapore toward an economic future where there is currently no available data and no pre-existing model to copy. If you look closely, what AI Singapore fundamentally does is technology forecasting. It operates as a continuous cycle at a national scale: collecting data, filtering it, analyzing the trends, spinning up strategic programs, observing the feedback, and figuring out what our next move needs to be. When AI Singapore was first formed in 2017, we started with very simple, tangible goals: publish 100 top-tier AI research papers, execute 100 applied industry projects, and launch "AI Grand Challenges" in collaboration with three key ministries: the Ministry of Health, the Ministry of National Development (which dealt with urban planning back then), and the Ministry of Education. It is not difficult to rationalize why; those were identified as the three most critical sectors for Singapore's immediate future. Those were our starting KPIs. Over time, as AI technology rapidly progressed, new strategic thrusts emerged. If you look at our complete operational picture today, tracing from left to right, it represents the entire evolution of a technology lifecycle. On the far left, you have AI as a very early-stage laboratory concept found in research papers. As you move to the right, you traverse through technology maturation, governance, product innovation, and finally AI talent development. That entire spectrum is the pipeline that I lead. A simpler way to understand it is that we have structured our national development efforts across the entire "value chain" of AI. You see, AI is ultimately just a piece of technology. If we cannot extract tangible value from it for Singapore's specific purposes—for our economic growth, for our research excellence, and for our workforce talent development—it remains nothing more than code. So, when looking at the complex slide behind me, simply recognize that we evaluate the value of AI across various domains: research, policy, platforms, business applications, and the talent pipeline. The success or failure of introducing any new technology rests heavily on whether it actually produces value. In the context of Singapore's economic model, that translates to: is there tangible business value being created? Consequently, we heavily focus on the right-hand side of that spectrum: platform value, business value, and talent pipelining. These three strategic thrusts are deeply anchored in the economic aspects of technology adoption. It is entirely about value creation and value capture. So, that gives you a high-level overview of where AI Singapore sits today.

    那么，在国家层面上进行技术远见意味着什么呢？这意味着我们已经完成的许多倡议，以及我们目前在新加坡人工智能计划（AI Singapore）中执行的事情，本质上都是技术预测的实践。当有人问我新加坡公共部门的生活是怎样的时，我经常开玩笑说：我们是专业的“水晶球预测家”。我们尽可能多、尽可能准确地凝视水晶球。我们吸收大量的信息——从专利研究，到与各个行业的深入对话，再到收集原始数据——因为我们的工作是引导新加坡这个国家走向一个目前没有可用数据、也没有现有模式可以复制的经济未来。如果你仔细观察，AI Singapore 根本上在做的就是技术预测。它在国家层面上作为一个连续的循环运作：收集数据，过滤数据，分析趋势，启动战略项目，观察反馈，并弄清楚我们的下一步应该怎么走。2017 年 AI Singapore 刚成立时，我们从非常简单、具体的目标开始：发表 100 篇顶级的 AI 研究论文，执行 100 个应用型工业项目，并与三个关键部委（卫生部、当时的国家发展部/城市规划，以及教育部）合作推出“人工智能大挑战（AI Grand Challenges）”。不难理解为什么；这些被认为是新加坡近期未来最关键的三个部门。这些是我们最初的 KPI。随着时间的推移，随着 AI 技术的快速进步，出现了新的战略方向。如果你看看我们今天的完整运营图景，从左到右，它代表了技术生命周期的整个演变。在最左边，AI 处于非常早期的实验室概念阶段，通常存在于研究论文中。向右移动，你将穿过技术成熟、治理、产品创新，最后是 AI 人才发展。这整个跨度就是我领导的管道。一个更简单的理解方式是，我们已经跨越 AI 的整个“价值链”构建了我们的国家发展工作。你看，AI 归根结底只是一项技术。如果我们不能为了新加坡的特定目的（为了我们的经济增长、研究卓越和劳动力人才发展）从中提取有形的价值，它就只不过是一堆代码。所以，当看我身后这张复杂的幻灯片时，只要认识到我们在各个领域评估 AI 的价值：研究、政策、平台、商业应用和人才管道。引入任何新技术的成功或失败很大程度上取决于它是否真的产生了价值。在新加坡经济模式的背景下，这转化为：是否创造了有形的商业价值？因此，我们非常重视该光谱的右侧：平台价值、商业价值和人才管道。这三个战略方向深深扎根于技术采用的经济层面。这完全关乎价值创造和价值捕获。这就是 AI Singapore 目前所处位置的一个高层次概述。

  - [18:23:43 - 18:28:02] **The Triple Helix Model and Technology Readiness Levels (TRL)**

    Now, I want to take you back to the beginning of our journey. I will frame this using academic research concepts, specifically how you look at technology foresighting supported by evidence-based technology adoption frameworks. In academic terms, AI Singapore operates as an "innovation intermediary." Since we are publicly funded by the Singapore government, we are precisely defined as a public innovation intermediary focused on artificial intelligence. In a knowledge-based economy, the classical theoretical model for a public innovation intermediary is known as the "Triple Helix" approach. The Triple Helix model describes a structured plan where the government brings academia and industry together to collaborate. Why is this specific structure necessary? When dealing with highly nascent, frontier technologies, traditional industries simply do not have the resident talent or risk appetite to innovate from scratch. In fact, most commercial industries strongly prefer technologies to reach a significant point of maturity before they engage, specifically so they face minimal financial and operational risk during experimentation and adoption. The deep know-how and expertise for frontier tech typically reside entirely on the academia side, which is exactly why you see technology transfer offices embedded within universities like NUS. So, where does the government come in? Organically, there are some economies that naturally foster aggressive interaction between academia and industry. The most famous example is the Silicon Valley environment in the United States, which features deep, privately funded partnerships between tech companies and universities. But there is a massive caveat to that model: it requires a specific class of highly capitalized companies willing to burn millions of dollars on R&D that may completely fail to yield a commercial outcome. What about the vast majority of companies in a nation that do not have such immense resources? That is exactly where the government steps in, completing the Triple Helix. The primary aim of the government in this model is to financially and structurally underwrite the risk of technology experimentation and adoption for these companies. The government supports the creation of an entity placed right in the center of academia and industry—the innovation intermediary. You can find tons of peer-reviewed research on Google Scholar regarding innovation intermediaries. In a classical intermediary model, you have "innovation performers" (like university labs) on one extreme, and "innovation adopters" (the industry companies) on the other. The intermediary sits right in the middle, managing the transfer across what we call the Technology Readiness Level, or TRL, scale. TRL is a standardized metric from 1 to 9. TRL 1 represents a raw concept first described in a research paper—typically just a mathematical equation. For the engineers here, this is basic first principles. TRL 1 to 3 involves demonstrating value purely in a research setting. TRL 7, 8, and 9 mean the technology is fully mature and is already embedded in everyday commercial products. The critical gap is TRL 4 to 6. This phase represents the incredibly difficult leap from a controlled lab environment into a viable, scalable commercial product. In the industry, we often ominously refer to TRL 4 to 6 as the "chasm of death," a concept you may have learned about in standard technology adoption lifecycles.

    现在，我想带大家回到我们旅程的起点。我将使用学术研究概念来构建这个框架，特别是如何看待由基于证据的技术采用框架所支持的技术远见。用学术术语来说，AI Singapore 作为一个“创新中介（innovation intermediary）”运作。由于我们是由新加坡政府公共资助的，我们被准确地定义为专注于人工智能的公共创新中介。在知识经济中，公共创新中介的经典理论模型被称为“三重螺旋（Triple Helix）”方法。三重螺旋模型描述了一个结构化的计划，政府在其中将学术界和工业界聚集在一起进行合作。为什么需要这种特定的结构呢？在处理非常早期的前沿技术时，传统行业根本没有内部人才或风险偏好来从头开始创新。事实上，大多数商业企业强烈倾向于在技术达到相当成熟的阶段后再介入，特别是为了在试验和采用过程中面临最小的财务和运营风险。前沿技术的深厚技术诀窍和专业知识通常完全存在于学术界，这正是为什么你会看到技术转让办公室设立在像 NUS 这样的大学内部。那么，政府在这个过程中扮演什么角色呢？在有机发展的情况下，某些经济体会自然而然地促进学术界和工业界之间的激烈互动。最著名的例子是美国的硅谷环境，其特点是科技公司和大学之间有着深厚的、由私人资助的合作关系。但这种模式有一个巨大的前提：它需要一类资本极其雄厚的公司，愿意在可能完全无法产生商业成果的研发上烧掉数百万美元。那么，对于一个国家中绝大多数没有如此庞大资源的公司来说呢？这正是政府介入的地方，从而完善了三重螺旋。在这个模型中，政府的主要目标是在财务和结构上为这些公司承担技术试验和采用的风险。政府支持在学术界和工业界的正中间建立一个实体——创新中介。你可以在谷歌学术上找到大量关于创新中介的同行评审研究。在一个经典的中介模型中，一端是“创新执行者”（如大学实验室），另一端是“创新采用者”（工业公司）。中介坐在正中间，管理着跨越我们所说的技术成熟度（Technology Readiness Level，简称 TRL）量表的转移。TRL 是一个从 1 到 9 的标准化指标。TRL 1 代表研究论文中首次描述的原始概念——通常只是一组数学方程。对于这里的工程师来说，这就是基本的第一性原理。TRL 1 到 3 纯粹是在研究环境中证明其价值。TRL 7、8 和 9 意味着技术已经完全成熟，并已嵌入日常商业产品中。关键的鸿沟是 TRL 4 到 6。这个阶段代表了从受控实验室环境到可行、可扩展的商业产品的极其艰难的跨越。在业界，我们经常有些不祥地将 TRL 4 到 6 称为“死亡之谷（chasm of death）”，你可能在标准的技术采用生命周期中学习过这个概念。

  - [18:28:02 - 18:35:30] **The Reality of AI Deployment: ML Ops and the Chasm of Death**

    So, the classical theory dictates that you take promising algorithms from academia, pass them through the intermediary to develop them for real-world problems, and hand them over to the adopters. However, this classical approach often fails miserably in practice. One of the harsh lessons we learned during our early industry projects back in 2017 was that even when we paired brilliant professors with eager companies, the final output was fundamentally unusable by the industry. Remember, back in 2017, the AI landscape was totally different. Amazon hadn't fully pivoted to AI services, Google Cloud's AI tools weren't prevalent, and generative models like ChatGPT didn't exist. Everything was hardcoded and manually fine-tuned. Professors would custom-train models on very clean, static datasets. The moment the company tried to plug that model into their messy, real-world production data, the model completely broke down. We quickly realized that there are massive, unique adoption challenges specific to AI that separate it from traditional software. Unfortunately, these challenges largely remain true today. If you look past the media hype and read properly conducted peer-reviewed research, actual, successful AI adoption rates in industry hover way below the 50th percentile. Even amidst today's absolute craze over Generative AI, a recent MIT study found that easily less than 5% of organizations are extracting sufficient, measurable business value from it. Why is this happening? Because academic research entirely focuses on tweaking the core machine learning algorithm. That small piece of code is where R&D thrives. But to extract actual business value, that model must be integrated into a massive, complex digital ecosystem. A famous framework by researchers like D. Sculley highlights the "technical debt" of AI systems. If you look at the backend of a platform like Netflix, the actual machine learning code is just a tiny fraction of the overarching infrastructure. Integrating and maintaining an AI model in the real world requires two critical capabilities that academic researchers typically do not possess: Operations (DevOps) to build and maintain the system infrastructure, and Data Engineering to manage continuous data pipelines and account for "model drift" as real-world data changes over time. Today, the fusion of these capabilities is known as Machine Learning Operations, or MLOps (sometimes called LLMOps in the context of large language models). MLOps is the strict engineering discipline of keeping an AI model alive and accurate in a chaotic production environment. When models hit real-world data, they inevitably drift and degrade. Most standard companies completely lack MLOps capabilities. The companies that excel at this are massive tech giants like Amazon AWS, Google Cloud, or Alibaba Cloud, because they live and die by managing immense data backends. Our first major revelation was this: if an AI model is not successfully put into stable production, absolutely zero real-world value is created. That realization forced a major pivot at AI Singapore just six months after we launched. Initially, we acted like traditional administrators, handing out grants and connecting people. We realized we had to build robust internal engineering capabilities. We transformed from a passive intermediary that just collected tech intelligence and advised on policy, into an active translation engine. We built our own deep engineering teams to ensure the deployability of AI systems. When we brought professors in to solve an industry problem, our engineers worked alongside them to elevate that raw code to the level of engineering maturity that commercial companies required. Without stepping in to cross the chasm of death, all our investment in technology foresighting and research would have remained a purely academic exercise with no economic impact.

    所以，经典理论表明，你从学术界获取有前景的算法，通过中介将它们开发出来以解决现实世界的问题，然后移交给采用者。然而，这种经典方法在实践中常常惨遭失败。我们在 2017 年早期工业项目中吸取的一个惨痛教训是，即使我们将杰出的教授与急切的公司配对，最终的输出结果在工业界也根本无法使用。记住，在 2017 年，AI 的格局完全不同。亚马逊还没有完全转向 AI 服务，谷歌云的 AI 工具还不普及，像 ChatGPT 这样的生成式模型也不存在。所有的东西都是硬编码和手动微调的。教授们会在非常干净、静态的数据集上定制训练模型。当公司试图将该模型接入其混乱的、真实世界的生产数据时，模型就彻底崩溃了。我们很快意识到，AI 面临着区别于传统软件的巨大、独特的采用挑战。不幸的是，这些挑战在今天很大程度上仍然存在。如果你抛开媒体的炒作，去阅读严谨的同行评审研究，你会发现工业界实际、成功的 AI 采用率徘徊在 50% 以下。即使在今天人们对生成式 AI 绝对狂热的背景下，麻省理工学院（MIT）最近的一项研究也发现，能够从中提取足够的、可衡量的商业价值的组织，比例轻易地不到 5%。为什么会这样？因为学术研究完全集中在调整核心机器学习算法上。那一小段代码是研发蓬勃发展的地方。但要提取实际的商业价值，该模型必须集成到一个庞大、复杂的数字生态系统中。像 D. Sculley 这样的研究人员提出的一个著名框架强调了 AI 系统的“技术债务（technical debt）”。如果你看看像 Netflix 这样的平台的后端，实际的机器学习代码只占整个基础设施的极小一部分。在现实世界中集成和维护 AI 模型需要学术研究人员通常不具备的两项关键能力：用于构建和维护系统基础设施的开发运维（DevOps），以及用于管理连续数据管道并解决由于现实世界数据随时间变化而导致的“模型漂移（model drift）”的数据工程（Data Engineering）。今天，这些能力的融合被称为机器学习运维，即 MLOps（在大型语言模型的背景下有时被称为 LLMOps）。MLOps 是一门严格的工程学科，旨在让 AI 模型在混乱的生产环境中保持活力和准确性。当模型接触到真实世界的数据时，它们不可避免地会发生漂移和退化。大多数普通公司完全缺乏 MLOps 能力。在这方面表现出色的公司是像亚马逊 AWS、谷歌云或阿里云这样的科技巨头，因为他们的生存和发展都依赖于管理庞大的数据后端。我们的第一个重大启示是：如果一个 AI 模型没有成功投入稳定生产，那么绝对不会创造任何现实世界的价值。这种认识迫使 AI Singapore 在成立仅仅六个月后就进行了重大战略调整。最初，我们就像传统的行政人员一样，发放资助并连接人员。我们意识到我们必须建立强大的内部工程能力。我们从一个仅仅收集技术情报和提供政策建议的被动中介，转变为一个活跃的转化引擎。我们建立了自己的深度工程团队，以确保 AI 系统的可部署性。当我们请教授来解决工业问题时，我们的工程师与他们并肩工作，将原始代码提升到商业公司所需的工程成熟度水平。如果不介入跨越这道“死亡之谷”，我们在技术远见和研究上的所有投资都将只是纯粹的学术演习，不会产生任何经济影响。

  - [18:35:30 - 18:43:29] **AI Readiness Index and the AI Apprenticeship Program**

    What specific steps have we taken over the past six years to actively shape the ecosystem? First, we had to acknowledge that not all companies are culturally or technologically ready to adopt AI. We needed a tool to prime companies and rapidly assess their operational readiness. So, we developed the AI Readiness Index, or ARI. If you Google "ARI AI Singapore," you can find it. It tests an organization across nine distinct dimensions and provides a relative score that only makes sense to the organization itself. Taking a 15-minute questionnaire tells a company if they are "AI Unaware," "AI Aware," "AI Ready," or "AI Competent." If an organization scores as "AI Unaware," we bluntly tell them to go skill up their workforce first. If they are "AI Aware," it suggests they shouldn't build custom AI but instead buy an off-the-shelf solution from the ecosystem of vendors we support. When a company tests as "AI Ready," that is when AI Singapore steps in to co-develop a custom solution with them over a rigorous six to nine-month framework. If they score as "AI Competent," we congratulate them but inform them they cannot tap into our grant funding because they are already capable of innovating on their own. After assessing readiness, we focused heavily on capacity building, starting with the industry workforce. Industry companies don't just need recent graduates; they desperately need experienced engineers who can hit the ground running. We needed a mechanism to take standard computer science graduates, or professionals pivoting careers, and rapidly inject real-world deployment skills into them. This led to the creation of the AI Apprenticeship Program (AIAP), which is now highly recognized in the local tech ecosystem. The AIAP is uniquely intense: working professionals must quit their current jobs to join us full-time for nine months. The government provides a living allowance during this period because these are adults with mortgages and mouths to feed. To get in, applicants must pass a grueling technical test that mirrors how top tech companies like Google or AWS interview. We don't just look at academic qualifications; candidates must code on the spot, explain their architectural choices in real-time, and articulate the 'why' behind their solutions. Over the nine months, apprentices work exclusively on real-world industry projects, not toy datasets. By the time they graduate, they have executed a real-world deployment, making them incredibly sought after. Currently, this program is exclusive to Singapore citizens. Here is a fascinating insight we discovered: a great industry AI engineer often does not come from a pure computer science background. A key differentiator between an average ML engineer and a great one is the ability to deeply understand the context of the data used for training. Unless you are building core AI architecture, most industry projects involve domains like healthcare, aviation, logistics, or creative media. An engineer with deep subject matter expertise in those specific fields understands the nuances of the data far better than a pure programmer. Therefore, while we still need CS graduates to invent groundbreaking new algorithms, the broader economy has an insatiable demand for people who know how to *apply* AI using deep domain knowledge. As we trained these engineers, we cultivated an agile development network. We strictly utilize Agile methodologies because technology foresighting is not static. You must constantly adjust your trajectory based on new insights. Over time, we packaged all this institutional knowledge on how to deploy AI effectively.

    在过去的六年里，我们采取了哪些具体步骤来积极塑造这个生态系统？首先，我们必须承认，并非所有公司在文化或技术上都准备好采用 AI。我们需要一个工具来引导公司并快速评估它们的运营准备情况。因此，我们开发了 AI 准备度指数（AI Readiness Index，简称 ARI）。如果你在谷歌上搜索“ARI AI Singapore”，就能找到它。它在九个不同的维度上测试一个组织，并提供一个只有对该组织本身才有意义的相对分数。完成一份 15 分钟的问卷后，公司就会知道他们是“缺乏 AI 意识（AI Unaware）”、“具备 AI 意识（AI Aware）”、“AI 准备就绪（AI Ready）”还是“AI 能力完备（AI Competent）”。如果一个组织得分为“缺乏 AI 意识”，我们会直截了当地告诉他们先去提升员工的技能。如果他们“具备 AI 意识”，这表明他们不应该定制开发 AI，而应该从我们支持的供应商生态系统中购买现成的解决方案。当公司测试为“AI 准备就绪”时，这就是 AI Singapore 介入的地方，我们会在一个严格的六到九个月的框架内与他们共同开发定制的解决方案。如果他们得分为“AI 能力完备”，我们会向他们表示祝贺，但也会告知他们不能动用我们的资助资金，因为他们已经具备了独立创新的能力。在评估了准备情况后，我们把重点放在能力建设上，从工业界劳动力开始。工业公司不仅需要应届毕业生；他们迫切需要能够迅速进入工作状态的经验丰富的工程师。我们需要一种机制，将标准的计算机科学毕业生，或者正在转换职业轨道的专业人士，迅速注入现实世界的部署技能。这就促成了 AI 学徒计划（AI Apprenticeship Program，简称 AIAP）的诞生，该计划目前在本地科技生态系统中享有很高的声誉。AIAP 的强度非常大：在职专业人士必须辞去当前的工作，全职加入我们九个月。政府在此时期提供生活津贴，因为这些都是需要还房贷、养家糊口的成年人。要进入该计划，申请人必须通过一项极其严格的技术测试，该测试模仿了像谷歌或 AWS 这样的顶级科技公司的面试方式。我们不仅仅看重学历；候选人必须当场编写代码，实时解释他们的架构选择，并清晰表达其解决方案背后的“原因”。在这九个月里，学徒们专门从事真实的行业项目，而不是玩具数据集。到他们毕业时，他们已经执行过一次真实的部署，这使得他们极其抢手。目前，该计划仅对新加坡公民开放。我们发现了一个有趣的现象：一个优秀的工业 AI 工程师通常并不具有纯粹的计算机科学背景。区分普通机器学习工程师和优秀工程师的一个关键点是：能否深刻理解用于训练的数据的背景。除非你正在构建核心的 AI 架构，否则大多数行业项目都涉及医疗保健、航空、物流或创意媒体等领域。在这些特定领域拥有深厚学科专业知识的工程师，比纯粹的程序员更了解数据的细微差别。因此，虽然我们仍然需要计算机科学毕业生来发明突破性的新算法，但更广泛的经济体对那些知道如何利用深厚的领域知识来“应用”AI 的人有着贪婪的需求。在培养这些工程师的过程中，我们建立了一个敏捷开发网络。我们严格采用敏捷（Agile）方法，因为技术远见不是静态的。你必须根据新的见解不断调整你的轨迹。随着时间的推移，我们将所有这些关于如何有效部署 AI 的机构知识打包在了一起。

  - [18:43:29 - 18:50:50] **Generative AI: Automation vs. Augmentation**

    Moving from industry to long-term talent pipelining, we needed to reach back one or two generations to ensure our educational systems were pivoting correctly. Around 2018, we built a comprehensive knowledge hub because there was no decent repository for structured AI training. We mapped out an intergenerational pipeline stretching from basic student literacy up to professional expertise. But as an innovation intermediary with our ear to the ground, we must constantly respond to paradigm shifts. The biggest shift arrived in November 2022 with the launch of ChatGPT and the subsequent explosion of Generative AI. Now, there is a pervasive narrative that "AI is taking our jobs." That framing is slightly inaccurate. It is not about AI directly replacing human roles; it is about how AI-assisted digital automation has exponentially increased, and that specific *automation* is disrupting the labor market. Goldman Sachs recently published data estimating that roughly a quarter of modern job tasks can be entirely automated by AI, heavily impacting administrative and support roles. While physical labor remains largely untouched, cognitive digital labor is in the crosshairs. But we must look deeper at how jobs are exposed to AI: is the exposure complementary, or is it a direct substitution? LinkedIn published a brilliant 2x2 matrix analyzing this. They categorized job exposure into three buckets: Insulated (no automation), Augmented (complementary AI tools), and Disrupted (total automation).

    Jobs requiring intense physical interaction or highly empathetic human connection remain insulated. Jobs with low cognitive complexity and high routine are prime candidates for total disruption. However, the surprise lies in roles requiring high cognition and intense creativity. You might assume digital creatives are doomed by text-to-image models. Yet, anecdotally, the opposite is occurring. Take Mighty Bear Production, a local company in Fusionopolis that designs 3D gaming assets. You would think Gen AI would ruin their business model. Instead, their clients still demand their specific expertise, but now Mighty Bear uses Gen AI to drastically increase productivity. Where it used to take immense manpower to produce five concept designs, they can now instantly generate unlimited permutations to show clients. Their human creativity is vastly *augmented* by AI, not replaced by it. When doing technology foresighting, visualizing data through matrices like this helps cut through the panic and reveals the true strategic landscape.

    从工业界转向长期的人才管道建设，我们需要向前追溯一两代人，以确保我们的教育系统正在正确地转向。大约在 2018 年，我们建立了一个综合知识中心，因为当时没有用于结构化 AI 培训的像样的资源库。我们规划了一个跨代管道，从学生的基础认知一直延伸到专业人员。但作为一个密切关注基层动态的创新中介，我们必须不断对范式转变做出反应。最大的转变发生在 2022 年 11 月，随着 ChatGPT 的发布以及随后生成式 AI 的爆发。现在，有一种普遍的说法是“AI 正在抢走我们的工作”。这种说法有些不太准确。这并不是说 AI 直接取代了人类的角色；而是关于 AI 辅助的数字自动化是如何呈指数级增长的，正是这种“自动化”正在颠覆劳动力市场。高盛最近公布的数据估计，大约四分之一的现代工作任务完全可以由 AI 自动化，这严重影响了行政和支持职位。虽然体力劳动在很大程度上未受影响，但认知型数字劳动却首当其冲。但我们必须更深入地研究工作是如何暴露在 AI 面前的：这种暴露是互补的，还是直接替代的？领英（LinkedIn）发布了一个出色的 2x2 矩阵来分析这个问题。他们将工作暴露程度分为三类：绝缘（Insulated，无自动化）、增强（Augmented，互补的 AI 工具）和颠覆（Disrupted，完全自动化）。需要大量物理互动或高度同理心的人际沟通的工作仍然是绝缘的。认知复杂性低且重复性高的工作是完全被颠覆的首选目标。然而，令人惊讶的是那些需要高认知和高创造力的职位。你可能会认为数字创意人员会被文本生成图像的模型逼上绝路。然而，从坊间传闻来看，情况恰恰相反。以 Fusionopolis 的一家设计 3D 游戏资产的本地公司 Mighty Bear Production 为例。你可能会认为生成式 AI 会毁了他们的商业模式。相反，他们的客户仍然需要他们的专业技能，但现在 Mighty Bear 使用生成式 AI 大幅提高了生产力。过去需要耗费大量人力才能制作出五个概念设计，现在他们可以瞬间生成无限的排列组合展示给客户。人类的创造力被 AI 极大地“增强”了，而不是被取代了。在进行技术远见规划时，通过像这样的矩阵将数据可视化，有助于消除恐慌，并揭示真实的战略格局。

  - [18:50:50 - 18:55:54] **National AI Strategy and Sea Lion LLM**

    These nuanced signals forced us to update our national playbook, leading to the National AI Strategy 2.0. My team specifically focuses on the "People and Community" pillar. We completely redefined the taxonomy of AI talent in Singapore. Previously, you were either an AI Creator (building algorithms) or an AI User (consuming the tech). We have now introduced a massive middle tier: the AI Practitioner. This is a domain expert—like an educator, a nurse, or a logistics manager—who fluently utilizes advanced AI tools to radically improve their specific workflows. We are tailoring national policies to rapidly expand this practitioner class, pushing initiatives like the International Olympiad in AI, which Singapore will host next year. We also focus heavily on capturing institutional knowledge. Because we are a sunsetting public entity—meaning our goal is for the ecosystem to eventually outgrow the need for us—we documented all our hard-learned lessons in the AI Singapore Engineers Playbook. But perhaps our most critical strategic pivot recently is the development of our own regional Large Language Model, named Sea Lion. Why are we spending millions to train our own LLM when OpenAI exists? It comes down to philosophy and cultural sovereignty. LLMs are incredibly efficient at Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG)—the core technology powering chatbots that retrieve information.

    But as younger generations increasingly rely on these tools, the LLM becomes their dominant source of baseline truth. If an LLM is engineered and fine-tuned exclusively by developers in Silicon Valley, it inherently reflects Western cultural values and biases. "Culture hijacking" through AI is a very real, insidious threat, even if it is not heavily discussed under standard AI ethics. We realized we could not have Western models unilaterally defining Asian cultural contexts. This realization triggered nations globally to build sovereign AI models—like G42 in the UAE or sovereign models in the Nordics. Because Singapore's linguistic footprint (like Singlish) is relatively small, we pooled GPU resources with Thailand, Indonesia, and other ASEAN neighbors to build Sea Lion as a representative Southeast Asian model. We then successfully lobbied giants like OpenAI and Meta to integrate Sea Lion as the baseline reference set for Asian cultural understanding within their global models. This is foresighting in action: anticipating a cultural threat and engineering a sovereign solution.

    这些微妙的信号迫使我们更新了国家战略，从而促成了国家人工智能战略 2.0（National AI Strategy 2.0）。我的团队特别关注“人员与社区”这一支柱。我们完全重新定义了新加坡 AI 人才的分类。以前，你不是 AI 创造者（构建算法），就是 AI 用户（消费技术）。我们现在引入了一个庞大的中间层：AI 从业人员（AI Practitioner）。这是指领域专家——比如教育工作者、护士或物流经理——他们熟练地利用先进的 AI 工具来彻底改善其特定的工作流程。我们正在调整国家政策，以迅速扩大这个从业者阶层，推动诸如国际人工智能奥林匹克竞赛（新加坡明年将主办）等活动。我们也非常注重捕捉机构知识。因为我们是一个最终会“日落（sunsetting）”的公共实体——意味着我们的目标是让生态系统最终成长到不再需要我们的地步——我们将所有来之不易的经验教训记录在了《AI Singapore 工程师手册（AI Singapore Engineers Playbook）》中。但也许我们最近最关键的战略转变是开发了我们自己的区域性大型语言模型，名为海狮（Sea Lion）。当 OpenAI 已经存在时，我们为什么要花费数百万美元来训练自己的 LLM 呢？这归结于哲学和文化主权。LLM 在检索增强生成（Retrieval-Augmented Generation，简称 RAG）方面极其高效——RAG 是驱动聊天机器人检索信息的核心技术。但随着年轻一代越来越依赖这些工具，LLM 成为他们获取基准真相的主要来源。如果一个 LLM 完全由硅谷的开发人员设计和微调，它本质上会反映西方的文化价值观和偏见。通过 AI 进行的“文化劫持（Culture hijacking）”是一个非常真实、潜伏的威胁，即使它在标准的 AI 伦理讨论中没有被重点提及。我们意识到，我们不能让西方模型单方面定义亚洲的文化语境。这种认识促使全球各个国家建立主权 AI 模型——比如阿联酋的 G42，或者北欧的主权模型。因为新加坡的语言足迹（比如 Singlish）相对较小，我们与泰国、印度尼西亚和其他东盟邻国汇集了 GPU 资源，以构建 Sea Lion 作为具有代表性的东南亚模型。然后，我们成功游说了像 OpenAI 和 Meta 这样的巨头，将 Sea Lion 整合为他们全球模型中理解亚洲文化的基准参考集。这就是行动中的技术远见：预见文化威胁并设计出主权的解决方案。

  - [18:55:54 - 18:59:38] **AI Standards and the Human Element**

    Finally, regarding AI governance and ethics, industry actors actually want to play by the rules, but they need to know what the rules are. This requires rigorous standardization. Singapore is an active, participating member of ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 42, the international standard committee dedicated to AI. It has rapidly become the most prolific standards committee globally. The critical number to remember here is ISO/IEC 42001. This is the global benchmark for AI management systems. It is pivotal, especially if you do business in Europe, because the newly passed EU AI Act functionally recognizes organizations certified under 42001 as broadly compliant with their strict regulations. We rely heavily on SC 42 rather than reinventing the wheel locally. Ultimately, despite all our strategies, infrastructure, and standards, you must remember one fundamental truth about technology forecasting: you cannot ignore the human element. Academic theories like Actor-Network Theory or the Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of Technology clearly demonstrate that even if a new technology mathematically proves to deliver massive economic and personal advantages, humans will still inherently resist change. The hardest part of our job isn't writing code; it is fundamentally changing organizational mindsets.

    最后，关于 AI 治理和伦理，行业参与者实际上是想按规矩办事的，但他们需要知道规矩是什么。这需要严格的标准化。新加坡是 ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 42（致力于 AI 的国际标准委员会）的积极参与成员。它已经迅速成为全球最多产的标准委员会。这里需要记住的关键数字是 ISO/IEC 42001。这是 AI 管理系统的全球基准。它至关重要，特别是如果你们在欧洲开展业务的话，因为新通过的欧盟《人工智能法案（EU AI Act）》在功能上认可通过 42001 认证的组织广泛符合其严格的法规。我们严重依赖 SC 42，而不是在本地重新发明轮子。归根结底，尽管我们有各种战略、基础设施和标准，你必须记住关于技术预测的一个基本事实：你不能忽视人的因素。像“行动者网络理论（Actor-Network Theory）”或“技术接受和使用统一理论（Unified Theory of Acceptance and Use of Technology）”这样的学术理论清楚地表明，即使一项新技术在数学上被证明能带来巨大的经济和个人优势，人类在本质上仍然会抗拒改变。我们工作中最难的部分不是写代码，而是从根本上改变组织的思维模式。

  - [18:59:38 - 19:07:19] **Q&A: Smart Nation, Regulated Industries, and AIAP Pivots**

    Let's address some questions. Someone asked about the distinction between Singapore's "Smart Nation" initiative and AI Singapore. Smart Nation was one of the founding agencies driving digital transformation. Structurally, Smart Nation now falls under the broader National AI Group, which merged with the Ministry of Communications and Information to form the new MDDI. AI Singapore functionally reports to this National AI Group, and they are responsible for authoring the overarching National AI Strategy frameworks. Another great question was regarding which industries are leading AI implementation and which are resistant. Naturally, the digital-native services sector is eagerly lapping it up. But the industries that are deliberately slow—not necessarily resistant, but highly cautious—are the strictly regulated industries. Globally, there are roughly seven deeply regulated domains: aviation, healthcare, logistics, power, and surprisingly to some, complex manufacturing. In these sectors, the cost of failure is catastrophic. Therefore, AI implementation will heavily favor "human-in-the-loop" systems rather than total autonomous control. That measured pace is actually a positive thing, and it is precisely where strict ISO standards come into play. Regarding the AI Apprenticeship Program (AIAP), a student asked about the background of the participants and where they end up. Do they return to their original industries to apply AI? Interestingly, no. Well over half of the participants join the program with the explicit mindset of executing a hard career pivot. They do not want to return to their old sectors. A significant proportion end up getting poached by major tech MNCs or highly funded AI startups. However, this still broadly benefits the ecosystem because they elevate the baseline of the "AI Practitioner" class across the entire economy. Finally, a question was raised regarding agility in our foresighting process given how fast AI changes. The secret to our national strategy is that we operate like a startup: we can spin up or sunset a major public program within three months. We don't stubbornly stick to failing roadmaps. The infrastructure components (like securing land for data centers) require rigid, long-term planning, but our talent and project execution pipelines are aggressively agile.

    让我们来回答一些问题。有人问到新加坡的“智慧国（Smart Nation）”倡议和 AI Singapore 之间的区别。智慧国是推动数字化转型的创始机构之一。在结构上，智慧国现在属于更广泛的国家人工智能组（National AI Group），该组与通讯及新闻部合并成立了新的数字发展及新闻部（MDDI）。AI Singapore 在职能上向这个国家人工智能组汇报，他们负责起草总体的国家人工智能战略框架。另一个很好的问题是关于哪些行业在引领 AI 的实施，哪些行业在抵制。自然地，数字原生服务领域正在热切地吸收它。但是，那些刻意放慢脚步的行业——不一定是抵制，而是高度谨慎——是受到严格监管的行业。在全球范围内，大约有七个受到深度监管的领域：航空、医疗保健、物流、电力，以及可能让一些人感到惊讶的复杂制造业。在这些领域，失败的代价是灾难性的。因此，AI 的实施将严重倾向于“人在回路（human-in-the-loop）”系统，而不是完全自主的控制。这种有分寸的步伐实际上是一件好事，这也是严格的 ISO 标准发挥作用的地方。关于 AI 学徒计划（AIAP），有同学问到参与者的背景以及他们的去向。他们会回到原来的行业应用 AI 吗？有趣的是，不会。超过一半的参与者加入该计划的心态是明确地执行一次艰难的职业转换。他们不想回到他们原来的行业。很大一部分人最终被大型科技跨国公司或资金雄厚的 AI 初创公司挖走。然而，这在广义上仍然有利于整个生态系统，因为他们提升了整个经济中“AI 从业人员”阶层的基准线。最后，鉴于 AI 变化如此之快，有人提出了关于我们在远见规划过程中的敏捷性的问题。我们国家战略的秘密在于，我们像一家初创公司一样运作：我们可以在三个月内启动或结束一个主要的公共项目。我们不会固执地坚持失败的路线图。基础设施组件（例如为数据中心获取土地）需要严格的长期规划，但我们的人才和项目执行管道具有极强的敏捷性。

  - [19:07:19 - 19:15:19] **Q&A: When Brilliant PhDs Fail in Industry**

    A student shared a fascinating, painful story from their own industry experience. Their company hired four top-tier AI PhD graduates from global universities to build a custom AI solution for a major client. Despite being brilliant researchers, the model they built completely failed to meet basic real-world requirements. The client lost millions of dollars, lost confidence in AI, and abandoned the project. The student asked for advice on how to avoid this. This is exactly why AI Singapore utilizes the "100 Experiments" (100E) agile framework! This story perfectly illustrates the disconnect I mentioned earlier. You "bought" talent assuming pure academic proficiency equates to business value. But a brilliant ML engineer without deep domain context is useless in a corporate setting. Conversely, if the business side cannot clearly articulate the specific operational problem they are trying to solve, failure is guaranteed. Every month, companies come to AI Singapore saying, "We want to do AI, tell us what to do." I immediately tell them to go home. The companies that succeed are those pinpointing a highly specific bottleneck. For example, an insurance company wanting to slash fraud detection time from 30 minutes to 5 minutes, or a gemstone company trying to sort 100 gems with one person instead of five. The sharper the business problem, the higher the success rate. Furthermore, you must force the business stakeholders to work alongside the engineers continuously. In our framework, the project sponsor must dedicate two internal staff members to work closely with our engineers on a strict three-week Agile sprint cycle. This prevents the PhDs from building something useless in a silo. The student then asked if data readiness is actually the most critical factor, noting that their clients often lack contiguous data pipelines. My blunt answer: if your client does not have robust data infrastructure, they are unequivocally *not* AI ready. You should not be selling them AI models. You should be selling them Data Engineers and performing basic Exploratory Data Analysis. Most corporate problems can be solved with clean operational data and simple analytics; they don't require deep learning models.

    有位同学分享了他们在业界经历的一个引人入胜却又痛苦的故事。他们的公司雇佣了四名来自全球顶尖大学的顶尖 AI 博士毕业生，为一位大客户构建定制的 AI 解决方案。尽管他们是杰出的研究人员，但他们构建的模型完全无法满足基本的现实世界需求。客户损失了数百万美元，对 AI 失去了信心，并放弃了该项目。这位同学询问如何避免这种情况的建议。这正是 AI Singapore 采用“100 个实验（100E）”敏捷框架的原因！这个故事完美地说明了我之前提到的脱节现象。你“购买”了人才，想当然地认为纯粹的学术熟练度就等同于商业价值。但是，在企业环境中，如果一个杰出的机器学习工程师没有深厚的领域背景，那就是毫无用处的。反过来说，如果业务方不能清楚地表达他们试图解决的具体运营问题，失败也是注定的。每个月都有公司来到 AI Singapore 说：“我们想做 AI，请告诉我们该怎么做。”我立刻叫他们回家。那些成功的公司是那些精确定位了高度特定的瓶颈的公司。例如，一家保险公司希望将欺诈检测时间从 30 分钟大幅削减到 5 分钟，或者一家宝石公司试图用一个人而不是五个人来分类 100 颗宝石。业务问题越尖锐，成功率就越高。此外，你必须强制业务利益相关者与工程师持续并肩工作。在我们的框架中，项目赞助商必须指派两名内部员工，在严格的三周敏捷冲刺周期内与我们的工程师密切合作。这可以防止博士们闭门造车，构建出无用的东西。随后这位同学问到，考虑到他们的客户通常缺乏连续的数据管道，数据准备度是否实际上是最关键的因素。我直言不讳的回答是：如果你的客户没有健全的数据基础设施，那么他们绝对*没有*做好 AI 的准备。你不应该向他们推销 AI 模型。你该向他们推销数据工程师，并执行基本的探索性数据分析（Exploratory Data Analysis）。大多数企业问题都可以通过清晰的运营数据和简单的分析来解决；它们根本不需要深度学习模型。

  - [19:15:19 - 19:19:57] **Q&A: Managing Client Expectations and Small Wins**

    Following up, the student pointed out the commercial reality: their tech consultancy makes money by selling solutions, so they cannot just bluntly tell paying clients to go away because they aren't "AI ready." How do you manage that while still delivering value? That is a classic tech sales dilemma. If a company demands AI but lacks the foundation, you must rapidly demonstrate localized value to build trust. Do not attempt a massive organizational overhaul. Pick a tiny, highly specific workflow that handles decent data—like an accounting process heavily reliant on Excel—and automate that. Deliver a fast, small prototype. Small wins build momentum and prove capability without risking catastrophic failure. Another student from ST Engineering jumped in to validate this, sharing their workflow: they conduct extensive user requirement gatherings first. When they discover a client isn't AI ready because processes are highly manual, they pivot the pitch to basic digitalization first, laying the groundwork while keeping the "vision" of AI alive as phase two. They use Solutions Architects to act as the crucial translators between vague client desires and strict engineering parameters. This aligns exactly with my point. Even massive Fortune 500 companies pour 98% of their tech budgets into basic digitalization and infrastructure, not complex AI modeling. You need project managers to carve the vision into palatable, deliverable chunks.

    紧接着，这位同学指出了商业现实：他们的科技咨询公司靠销售解决方案赚钱，所以他们不能直白地告诉付费客户走开，仅仅因为他们“没有做好 AI 的准备”。你如何在提供价值的同时处理这个问题？这是一个经典的科技销售困境。如果一家公司要求使用 AI 但缺乏基础，你必须迅速展示局部的价值以建立信任。不要试图进行大规模的组织机构大修。挑选一个处理尚可数据的、高度特定的小型工作流程——比如严重依赖 Excel 的会计流程——并将其自动化。交付一个快速、小型的原型。小小的胜利可以建立势头并证明能力，而不会冒灾难性失败的风险。另一位来自 ST Engineering 的同学插话证实了这一点，分享了他们的工作流程：他们首先进行广泛的用户需求收集。当他们发现客户因为流程高度依赖人工而没有做好 AI 准备时，他们会调整推销策略，首先进行基础的数字化，在打下基础的同时，将 AI 的“愿景”作为第二阶段保留下来。他们利用解决方案架构师（Solutions Architects）作为模糊的客户愿望和严格的工程参数之间关键的“翻译者”。这与我的观点完全一致。即使是庞大的财富 500 强公司，也会将 98% 的技术预算投入到基础的数字化和基础设施中，而不是复杂的 AI 建模。你需要项目经理将愿景分割成容易接受、可交付的模块。

  - [19:19:57 - 19:24:13] **Q&A: Singapore's Strategic Positioning in AI Infrastructure**

    Another student noticed that AI Singapore's strategy doesn't heavily emphasize core hardware manufacturing. They asked about Singapore's strategy regarding semiconductor chips, noting we don't produce cutting-edge sub-nanometer chips like TSMC, nor do we build the extreme ultraviolet lithography (EUV) machines uniquely manufactured by ASML. That is a loaded geopolitical and economic question. From a public sector perspective, Singapore is positioning itself as a strategic, stabilizing node in the global chip supply chain, but we are not trying to compete in front-end cutting-edge fabrication. Not all chips are advanced Nvidia GPUs; we have robust wafer fabs in areas like Pasir Ris handling mature node manufacturing. More crucially, regarding the "Hyperscalers" (the massive data centers housing vast AI compute clusters), Singapore is severely constrained by physical land and energy capacity. Hyperscalers devour electricity and require dedicated water cooling lines. We have to make a very conscious, difficult trade-off about sacrificing precious land. Instead of building endless data centers locally, we leverage our geographic position. Huge hyperscalers are being built just across the border in Johor Bahru (JB). Since Singapore invested heavily years ago to become the premier landing hub for submarine optical cables, we can pipe that computing power across the border with minimal latency. Personally, I believe Singapore does not need to enter the arms race of training foundational models. Foundational Large Language Models are becoming commoditized, and the research frontier is already shifting toward "World Models" and spatial intelligence championed by experts like Dr. Fei-Fei Li. Training those requires insane compute power. The real economic value for Singapore lies in *inferencing*—actually running the pre-trained models. As offline inference models running directly on mobile chips and ASICs become prevalent, the infrastructure demand shifts. We don't need to over-invest in training infrastructure. Our strategic advantage is being the global gold standard for AI policy, governance, and applied business ecosystems.

    另一位同学注意到，AI Singapore 的战略并没有过度强调核心硬件制造。他们询问了新加坡在半导体芯片方面的战略，并指出我们既不生产像台积电（TSMC）那样尖端的亚纳米级芯片，也不制造由 ASML 独家制造的极紫外（EUV）光刻机。这是一个包含地缘政治和经济因素的重磅问题。从公共部门的角度来看，新加坡正将自己定位为全球芯片供应链中一个具有战略意义的稳定节点，但我们并不试图在前端尖端制造领域竞争。并非所有的芯片都是先进的英伟达（Nvidia）GPU；我们在巴西立（Pasir Ris）等地拥有强大的晶圆厂，处理成熟节点的制造。更关键的是，关于“超大规模数据中心（Hyperscalers）”（容纳庞大 AI 计算集群的巨型数据中心），新加坡在物理土地和能源容量方面受到严重限制。超大规模数据中心会吞噬大量电力，并需要专用的水冷管线。在牺牲宝贵土地的问题上，我们必须做出非常谨慎、艰难的权衡。我们没有在本地无休止地建设数据中心，而是利用我们的地理位置优势。巨大的超大规模数据中心正在一水之隔的柔佛州新山（JB）建设。由于新加坡多年前投入巨资，成为海底光缆的首要登陆枢纽，我们可以将这些计算能力跨越国界传输，且延迟极低。就我个人而言，我认为新加坡不需要卷入训练基础模型的军备竞赛。基础的大型语言模型正变得商品化，而研究的前沿已经转向了由李飞飞博士等专家倡导的“世界模型（World Models）”和空间智能。训练这些模型需要极其疯狂的计算能力。对新加坡来说，真正的经济价值在于“推理（inferencing）”——也就是实际运行预先训练好的模型。随着直接在移动芯片和专用集成电路（ASIC）上运行的离线推理模型变得普遍，对基础设施的需求也会发生转变。我们不需要在训练基础设施上过度投资。我们的战略优势在于成为 AI 政策、治理和应用商业生态系统的全球黄金标准。

  - [19:24:13 - 19:28:05] **Q&A: Exiting Programs and Shifting Metrics**

    Kai asked a final question regarding the lifecycle of our initiatives: When acting as an innovation intermediary, what specific indices or benchmarks tell you it is time to pivot, scale down, or completely exit an ecosystem program? Excellent question. I'll give you a glimpse under the hood of public sector governance. We establish initiatives to absorb the initial risk of tech adoption. The ultimate signal to exit is market commoditization. The moment a technology becomes democratized and cheap enough that private industries willingly invest their own money, the government seizes funding and redirects it elsewhere. Academia is highly effective for early-stage TRL research, but struggles with commercial deployment, so academic grants taper off as tech matures. But how do we strictly measure this? Like any enterprise, we use KPIs. At the inception of a national program, we set hard numerical targets on a two to three-year cycle—such as the number of trained engineers or deployed models. These raw numbers serve as a proxy for the baseline adoption rate. However, as the ecosystem matures, tracking raw volume becomes meaningless. In our recent cycles, we aggressively pivoted our KPIs away from numerical quantity toward qualitative *impact*. My roadmap for the next five years is almost entirely devoid of volume metrics; it is entirely focused on the depth of economic value created by the adoption. If the impact stabilizes, it signals the industry is fully self-serving, and AI Singapore's intervention in that specific vector can successfully sunset. You must align your measurement metrics strictly with your evolving strategic hypotheses. Thank you all for the excellent questions!

    Kai 提出了最后一个关于我们项目生命周期的问题：在作为创新中介时，什么具体的指标或基准会告诉你，是时候转变、缩减规模或完全退出一个生态系统项目了？非常好的问题。我给大家透露一下公共部门治理的内部情况。我们建立倡议的目的是吸收技术采用的初期风险。退出的最终信号是市场商品化。当一项技术变得足够普及和廉价，私营企业愿意自掏腰包投资时，政府就会切断资金并将其重新引导到其他地方。学术界对于早期 TRL 研究非常有效，但在商业部署方面却举步维艰，因此随着技术的成熟，学术资助会逐渐减少。但我们如何严格衡量这一点呢？像任何企业一样，我们使用 KPI。在一个国家项目启动时，我们会设定两到三年为一个周期的硬性数字目标——例如培训的工程师数量或部署的模型数量。这些原始数字可以作为基准采用率的代表。然而，随着生态系统的成熟，追踪原始数量变得毫无意义。在我们最近的周期中，我们积极地将 KPI 从数字上的数量转向了定性的“影响（impact）”。我未来五年的路线图几乎完全没有数量指标；它完全集中在采用所创造的经济价值的深度上。如果影响稳定下来，就表明该行业已经完全能够自我服务，AI Singapore 在该特定方向上的干预就可以成功“日落”。你必须使你的衡量指标与你不断演变的战略假设严格对齐。感谢大家提出这些精彩的问题！

When you are constantly putting out fires today, you simply do not have the operational luxury or the capital to look ten years down the road.

The Benchmarking Report explicitly classifies firms based on their Foresight Need versus their Foresight Maturity, showing that a massive percentage of firms are "In Danger" because they focus only on current metrics while driving blind into the future.
